We have A = (x_A, y_A). P = (x_P, y_P). v = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). So:

v·A = (x_P - x_A) x_A + (y_P - y_A) y_A = x_P x_A - x_A^2 + y_P y_A - y_A^2 = x_P x_A + y_P y_A - (x_A^2+y_A^2) = x_P x_A + y_P y_A - r^2.

But we can also compute v·(A-N) = v·A - v·N? Because v·(A-N)=v·A - v·N. Since N=(1,0), v·N = (x_P - x_A)*1 + (y_P - y_A)*0 = x_P - x_A. So v·(A-N) = v·A - (x_P - x_A). That matches: (x_P x_A + y_P y_A - r^2) - (x_P - x_A) = x_P x_A + y_P y_A - r^2 - x_P + x_A. Good.

Now we need to compute t_E and t_F. So t_E = -2 (v·A)/|v|^2 = -2 (x_P x_A + y_P y_A - r^2)/|v|^2.

Alternatively, we can use complex numbers with the line MN as real axis. Let M=0, N=1 (after scaling). Then circles: |z| = r, |z-1| = R. Intersection points A and B: A = a, B = conjugate? Given the complexity, analytic geometry seems plausible. Let's try to compute the tangency condition analytically.

Set d=1. Use coordinates:

M = (0,0), N = (1,0). r and R with r<R, and 1 > R-r? Actually, from intersection: |R-r| < 1 < R+r. So R-r < 1 < R+r. So we have R < r+1, and R > r? Actually, R > r, so R-r positive, and less than 1.

Let A = (x, y) with y>0, x = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2, y = sqrt(r^2 - x^2). Also, B = (x, -y).

P = (p, q) with p = (1+R-r)/2, q = -p(r+x)/y.

H = (p, y_H) with y_H = - (1-p) y/(r+x) because earlier we derived y_H = - (1-p) y/(r+x). Check: (1-p) = (1 - (1+R-r)/2) = (2-1-R+r)/2 = (1-R+r)/2 = (r+1-R)/2. Since R<r+1, 1-p>0. So y_H = - ((1-p) y)/(r+x). Good.

Now line AP: direction v = (p-x, q-y). We'll compute t_E and t_F.

First, compute v·A = (p-x)x + (q-y)y = p x - x^2 + q y - y^2 = p x + q y - (x^2+y^2) = p x + q y - r^2.

Now we need p x + q y. We have q = -p(r+x)/y. So q y = -p(r+x). So p x + q y = p x - p(r+x) = p x - p r - p x = -p r. So p x + q y = -p r. Therefore, v·A = -p r - r^2 = -r(p + r). So v·A = -r(p+r). Good!

Thus t_E = -2 (v·A)/|v|^2 = -2 * (-r(p+r))/|v|^2 = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2.

So E = A + t_E v = A + (2r(p+r)/|v|^2) v.

Now compute v·(A-N). Since N=(1,0), A-N = (x-1, y). Then v·(A-N) = (p-x)(x-1) + (q-y)y = (p-x)(x-1) + q y - y^2 = (p-x)(x-1) + q y - y^2.

We can compute q y = -p(r+x). And y^2 = r^2 - x^2. So:

v·(A-N) = (p-x)(x-1) - p(r+x) - (r^2 - x^2) = (p-x)(x-1) - p r - p x - r^2 + x^2.

Now expand (p-x)(x-1) = p(x-1) - x(x-1) = p x - p - x^2 + x. So:

v·(A-N) = (p x - p - x^2 + x) - p r - p x - r^2 + x^2 = p x - p - x^2 + x - p r - p x - r^2 + x^2 = -p - p r + x - r^2 = -(p(1+r)) + r^2 - x).

But note that p = (1+R-r)/2. Also, we might find a simpler expression: v·(A-N) = v·A - v·N = v·A - (p-x) because v·N = (p-x)*1 + (q-y)*0 = p-x. So v·(A-N) = v·A - (p-x) = -r(p+r) - (p-x) = -r(p+r) - p + x = -(p r + r^2 + p - x) = -(p(1+r) + r^2 - x). Same.

Now we need to compute t_F = -2 Re((A-1) \overline{v})/|v|^2. But in real coordinates, t_F = -2 (v·(A-N))/|v|^2. So t_F = -2 * (v·(A-N))/|v|^2 = -2 * ( -(p(1+r)+r^2 - x) )/|v|^2 = 2(p(1+r)+r^2 - x)/|v|^2.

Thus F = A + t_F v = A + (2(p(1+r)+r^2 - x)/|v|^2) v.

Now we have E and F expressed in terms of A and v, with t_E and t_F.

Now we need to consider circle (BEF). Points: B = (x, -y), E, F. Since E and F are on line AP, and B is symmetric to A across x-axis, maybe we can find the equation of the circle through B, E, F.

Then points E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. So we have three points: B, A+t_E v, A+t_F v. We can find the circle through them. Because of the symmetry in the parameters, perhaps the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle through A, E, F reflected across the x-axis? Actually, if we reflect the circle (AEF) across x-axis, we get a circle through B and the reflections of E and F. But the reflections of E and F are not E and F; they are points on the reflected line of AP. However, note that the line AP, when reflected across x-axis, becomes line through B and the reflection of P? Let's denote P' = reflection of P across x-axis: P' = (p, -q). Since q negative, -q positive. Then line AP reflected is line through B and P'? But A reflected is B, and P reflected is P', so the reflected line is BP'. The points E and F on line AP reflect to points E' and F' on line BP'. So the circle through B, E, F is not the reflection of (AEF) unless E'=E and F'=F, which would require that line AP is symmetric about x-axis, i.e., v is vertical? Not generally.

So that's not.

Maybe we can compute the circumcircle of BEF using the concept of the circle through B and two points on a line. There is a known formula: If we have points B and two points X, Y on line L (through A), then the center O is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of XY and the perpendicular bisector of BX. The perpendicular bisector of XY is a line perpendicular to L through the midpoint M_XY. The perpendicular bisector of BX is a line perpendicular to BX through its midpoint. This can be expressed using vectors. Possibly we can find O in terms of A, B, and the parameter t. Since E and F are symmetric in t_E and t_F? Not symmetric.

Maybe we can find that the circumcircle of BEF has equation: (x - x0)^2 + (y + y0)^2 = R^2, where x0 and y0 are something like ( (p+r)^2? Not.

Given the algebraic nature, we might try to compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent if and only if the power of H equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but we need a direct relation.

Alternatively, we can compute the condition for tangency directly: For line L: H + s v, we need that the circle (BEF) and L have a double intersection. So we need to show that the quadratic in s from plugging H+sv into circle equation has discriminant zero. That is, (v·(H-O))^2 = |v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2). So if we can find O and R in terms of known quantities, we can compute this.

Maybe we can find that O is the midpoint of something like the arc? Or maybe O lies on the circle with diameter? Could be that O is the reflection of H across the midpoint of something? Let's compute O in our numeric example more precisely exactly using rational numbers.

Maybe we can use coordinate geometry to prove the tangency condition. Since we already have a numeric example that suggests O lies on x=1/2, we can try to compute O_x in general using algebraic manipulation. Let's attempt to compute O_x by solving for the circumcenter of B, E, F in terms of a, p, q, etc.

Define:

Let A = (x, y) with y>0.
Let B = (x, -y).
Let v = (v_x, v_y) = (p - x, q - y). We have p = (1+R-r)/2, q = -p(r+x)/y.

We also have t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2, t_F = 2(p(1+r)+r^2-x)/|v|^2.

Then points:

E = (x + t_E v_x, y + t_E v_y)
F = (x + t_F v_x, y + t_F v_y)

Now, the circumcenter O can be found by solving the system:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2.

From |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2, we get:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - (x + t_E v_x))^2 + (O_y - (y + t_E v_y))^2

Expand:

(O_x^2 -2x O_x + x^2) + (O_y^2 +2y O_y + y^2) = (O_x^2 -2(x + t_E v_x) O_x + (x + t_E v_x)^2) + (O_y^2 -2(y + t_E v_y) O_y + (y + t_E v_y)^2)

Cancel O_x^2 and O_y^2:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = -2(x + t_E v_x) O_x + (x + t_E v_x)^2 -2(y + t_E v_y) O_y + (y + t_E v_y)^2

Bring terms:

-2x O_x +2(x + t_E v_x) O_x +2y O_y +2(y + t_E v_y) O_y = (x + t_E v_x)^2 + (y + t_E v_y)^2 - (x^2+y^2)

Simplify left: 2 t_E v_x O_x + 2 (2y + t_E v_y?) Wait, careful: -2x O_x +2(x + t_E v_x) O_x = 2 t_E v_x O_x. And 2y O_y +2(y + t_E v_y) O_y = 2y O_y +2y O_y +2 t_E v_y O_y = 4y O_y + 2 t_E v_y O_y. So left side = 2 t_E v_x O_x + (4y + 2 t_E v_y) O_y.

Right side: (x^2 +2x t_E v_x + t_E^2 v_x^2) + (y^2 +2y t_E v_y + t_E^2 v_y^2) - (x^2+y^2) = 2x t_E v_x + t_E^2 v_x^2 + 2y t_E v_y + t_E^2 v_y^2 = 2 t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + t_E^2 |v|^2.

Thus:

2 t_E v_x O_x + (4y + 2 t_E v_y) O_y = 2 t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + t_E^2 |v|^2.

Divide by 2: t_E v_x O_x + (2y + t_E v_y) O_y = t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) |v|^2.   (Eq1)

Similarly, from |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2, we get:

t_F v_x O_x + (2y + t_F v_y) O_y = t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_F^2/2) |v|^2.   (Eq2)

Now we have two linear equations in O_x, O_y. We can solve for O_x, O_y. Subtract Eq1 and Eq2? Actually, we can solve the system.

Let’s denote:

A1 = t_E v_x, B1 = 2y + t_E v_y, C1 = t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) |v|^2.
A2 = t_F v_x, B2 = 2y + t_F v_y, C2 = t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_F^2/2) |v|^2.

Then we have:

A1 O_x + B1 O_y = C1
A2 O_x + B2 O_y = C2

Solve for O_x and O_y.

We can compute O_x = (C1 B2 - C2 B1) / (A1 B2 - A2 B1). We want to show that O_x = 1/2.

Now, note that v_x = p - x, v_y = q - y. And we have p, q in terms of r, R, etc. Also, x v_x + y v_y = x(p-x) + y(q-y) = p x - x^2 + q y - y^2 = (p x + q y) - (x^2+y^2) = (p x + q y) - r^2. But we earlier computed p x + q y = -p r. So x v_x + y v_y = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So that's a known expression.

Also, |v|^2 = (p+r)^2+q^2.

Now, t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2, t_F = 2(p(1+r)+r^2 - x)/|v|^2.

We need to compute A1, B1, C1, etc. Let's denote D = |v|^2. Then t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, t_F = 2S/D, where S = p(1+r)+r^2 - x.

Then:

A1 = t_E v_x = (2r(p+r)/D) * v_x.
B1 = 2y + t_E v_y = 2y + (2r(p+r)/D) v_y.
C1 = t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) D = (2r(p+r)/D) * (-r(p+r)) + ( (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 ) * D = -2r^2(p+r)^2/D + (2r^2(p+r)^2/D^2) * D? Wait, t_E^2/2 * D = ( (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 ) * D = (2r^2(p+r)^2/D^2) * D = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So C1 = -2r^2(p+r)^2/D + 2r^2(p+r)^2/D = 0. So C1 = 0. That's great! So the equation simplifies to:

A1 O_x + B1 O_y = 0. (1)

Similarly, C2 = t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_F^2/2) D = (2S/D) * (-r(p+r)) + ( (4S^2/D^2)/2 ) * D = -2S r(p+r)/D + (2S^2/D^2) * D = -2S r(p+r)/D + 2S^2/D = (2S/D)(S - r(p+r)). So C2 = (2S/D)(S - r(p+r)). Now we need to compute S - r(p+r). S = p(1+r)+r^2 - x. So S - r(p+r) = p(1+r)+r^2 - x - r p - r^2 = p(1+r) - r p - x = p + pr - rp - x = p - x. So S - r(p+r) = p - x = v_x. Thus C2 = (2S/D) * v_x. So C2 = 2S v_x / D.

Now we have:

(1) A1 O_x + B1 O_y = 0.
(2) A2 O_x + B2 O_y = C2.

Now, A1 = (2r(p+r)/D) v_x, B1 = 2y + (2r(p+r)/D) v_y. A2 = (2S/D) v_x, B2 = 2y + (2S/D) v_y.

We can divide both equations by 2 maybe. Let's write:

(1) (r(p+r) v_x / D) O_x + (y + (r(p+r) v_y / D)) O_y = 0.
(2) (S v_x / D) O_x + (y + (S v_y / D)) O_y = C2 = 2S v_x / D.

Now, note that v_x and v_y are known; also, r(p+r) and S are related. We can try to solve for O_x, O_y. Let's denote:

U = r(p+r)/D, V = S/D. Then:

(1) U v_x O_x + (y + U v_y) O_y = 0.
(2) V v_x O_x + (y + V v_y) O_y = 2V v_x.

Now we can solve these two linear equations.

Let’s write them as:

U v_x O_x + (y + U v_y) O_y = 0   (1)
V v_x O_x + (y + V v_y) O_y = 2V v_x   (2)

We can solve for O_x and O_y. Multiply (1) by (y+V v_y) and (2) by (y+U v_y) and subtract? Or use elimination.

Let’s treat O_x and O_y as unknowns. Write in matrix form:

[ U v_x, y+U v_y ] [O_x; O_y] = [0]
[ V v_x, y+V v_y ] [O_x; O_y] = [2V v_x]

Solve using Cramer's rule.

Determinant Δ = (U v_x)(y+V v_y) - (V v_x)(y+U v_y) = v_x [ U(y+V v_y) - V(y+U v_y) ] = v_x [ U y + U V v_y - V y - U V v_y ] = v_x [ (U - V) y ] = v_x y (U - V).

So Δ = v_x y (U - V).

Now, O_x = (0*(y+V v_y) - (2V v_x)*(y+U v_y)) / Δ = - (2V v_x)(y+U v_y) / (v_x y (U - V)) = -2V (y+U v_y) / (y (U - V)).

Similarly, O_y = ( (U v_x)*(2V v_x) - 0*(V v_x) ) / Δ = (2U V v_x^2) / (v_x y (U - V)) = (2U V v_x) / (y (U - V)).

We want O_x = 1/2. So we need to verify that:

-2V (y+U v_y) / (y (U - V)) = 1/2 => -4V (y+U v_y) = y (U - V).   (EqX)

Similarly, we might also have O_y expression, but we need to prove EqX holds.

Now, we have:

U = r(p+r)/D, V = S/D.

So U - V = (r(p+r) - S)/D.

Now, y+U v_y = y + (r(p+r)/D) v_y.

Thus we need to check:

-4V (y+U v_y) = y (U - V).

Plug V = S/D, U = r(p+r)/D, and D = (p+r)^2+q^2. Also, v_y = q - y. And we have relation from earlier: q = -p(r+x)/y. So v_y = -p(r+x)/y - y = -(p(r+x)+y^2)/y. And y^2 = r^2 - x^2.

We also have S = p(1+r)+r^2 - x.

Let's try to compute U - V and y+U v_y in terms of r, p, x, y, and maybe simplify using known relations.

We have D = (p+r)^2+q^2. But also, we have q^2 =? From q = -p(r+x)/y, so q^2 = p^2(r+x)^2/y^2. And y^2 = r^2 - x^2. So D = (p+r)^2 + p^2(r+x)^2/y^2.

Now, r(p+r) - S = r(p+r) - [p(1+r)+r^2 - x] = rp + r^2 - p - pr - r^2 + x = -p + x = -(p - x) = -v_x. So r(p+r) - S = -v_x. Thus U - V = (r(p+r)-S)/D = -v_x/D.

Thus U - V = -v_x/D.

Now, y+U v_y = y + (r(p+r)/D) v_y.

So EqX: -4V (y+U v_y) = y (U - V) => -4*(S/D)*(y + (r(p+r)/D v_y) = y * (-v_x/D) => Multiply both sides by D: -4S (y + (r(p+r)/D v_y) = -y v_x => Multiply by -1: 4S (y + (r(p+r)/D v_y) = y v_x.

Thus we need to show:

4S y + 4S * (r(p+r)/D) v_y = y v_x.

Or equivalently:

4S y + (4S r(p+r)/D) v_y = y v_x.

Now, note that v_x = p - x. And we have S = p(1+r)+r^2 - x. Also, v_y = q - y = -p(r+x)/y - y = -(p(r+x)+y^2)/y = -(p(r+x)+r^2-x^2)/y = -(p r + p x + r^2 - x^2)/y.

This looks messy but we can try to simplify using the relation between x, y, r. Also, from the circle equations: x = (1+r^2-R^2)/2, and y^2 = r^2 - x^2. Also, R is related to p? Since p = (1+R-r)/2, we have R = 2p - 1 + r. Also, from x = (1+r^2-R^2)/2, we can express x in terms of r and p: R = 2p - 1 + r, so R^2 = (2p - 1 + r)^2 = 4p^2 + 1 + r^2 -4p +4pr -2r? Actually, expand: (2p + r -1)^2 = (2p + r)^2 -2(2p+r) +1 = 4p^2 +4pr + r^2 -4p -2r +1. Compute S = 2p^2+3p(A-p)+(A-p)^2 - p - (A-p) = 2p^2+3pA-3p^2 + (A^2-2Ap+p^2) - p - A + p = (2p^2-3p^2+p^2) + (3pA-2Ap) + A^2 - p - A + p = 0 + pA + A^2 - A = A(p+A-1) = A(p + A -1). But A = p+r, so p+A-1 = p+p+r-1 = 2p+r-1. So S = A(2p+r-1). That's nice! Because we had earlier that v·O = S, and we also had v·H = p( (2p+1)A -1). So v·H - S = p( (2p+1)A -1) - A(2p+r-1). But we can also express r = A-p. So 2p+r-1 = 2p + A - p -1 = p + A -1. So S = A(p+A-1). Good.

Now, we also have v·H = p( (2p+1)A -1 ) = p(2pA+A-1) = p( A(2p+1) -1). So v·H - S = p( A(2p+1) -1) - A(p+A-1) = pA(2p+1) - p - Ap - A^2 + A = pA(2p+1-1) - p - A^2 + A = pA(2p) - p - A^2 + A = 2p^2A - p - A^2 + A. So v·H - S = 2p^2A - p - A^2 + A. But A = p+r, so this is 2p^2(p+r) - p - (p+r)^2 + (p+r) = 2p^3+2p^2r - p - (p^2+2pr+r^2) + p+r = 2p^3+2p^2r - p - p^2 -2pr - r^2 + p + r = 2p^3 - p^2 + 2p^2r -2pr - r^2 + r. That's what we had. So it's not zero.

Now, we need to compute the discriminant condition. Let's try to compute |H-B|^2 - |O-B|^2 directly using the expressions we have for H and B and O. Since we have O_x = 1/2? We haven't proven that, but maybe we can compute O_x from the linear equations using the expressions for v, y, etc. Actually, we can solve for O directly using the formulas we derived earlier: O_x = S/(y v_x) * (y + U v_y). But we can also compute O from the system: Since O satisfies v·O = S, and also O lies on perpendicular bisector of BE. Maybe we can find O explicitly in terms of p and r.

Given that we have expressions for v, y, v_x, etc., we can compute O_x and O_y. Let's try to compute O using the fact that O is the circumcenter of B, E, F. Since B=(x,-y), and E,F on line AP, we can also use the concept of the circle through B and two points on a line. We can compute O_x by using the formula for the circumcenter in coordinates. There is a formula: For points (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3), the circumcenter can be computed using determinants. But maybe we can find a simpler relation: Since we already have v·O = S, and also O_x =? Let's try to compute O_y from the first equation: U v_x O_x + (y+U v_y) O_y = 0. But we also have from the second equation: V v_x O_x + (y+V v_y) O_y = V v_x. Using these, we can solve for O_x and O_y in terms of parameters. Let's do that.

We have:

(1) U v_x O_x + (y+U v_y) O_y = 0
(2) V v_x O_x + (y+V v_y) O_y = V v_x

We can solve for O_x and O_y using elimination. Write in matrix form:

[ U v_x, y+U v_y ] [O_x; O_y] = [0]
[ V v_x, y+V v_y ] [O_x; O_y] = [V v_x]

Solve for O_x using Cramer's rule:

Δ = (U v_x)(y+V v_y) - (V v_x)(y+U v_y) = v_x[ U(y+V v_y) - V(y+U v_y) ] = v_x[ Uy + UV v_y - Vy - UV v_y ] = v_x[ (U-V)y ] = v_x y (U-V). As before.

Now, O_x = (0*(y+V v_y) - (V v_x)*(y+U v_y)) / Δ = - V v_x (y+U v_y) / (v_x y (U-V)) = - V (y+U v_y) / (y (U-V)).

Now, U-V = (r(p+r)/D - S/D) = (rA - S)/D. But we already have rA - S =? Since S = A(p+A-1) = A(p+A-1), and rA = A r, so rA - S = A(r - (p+A-1)) = A(r - p - A +1) = A(r - p - (p+r) +1) = A(r - p - p - r +1) = A(-2p+1) = A(1-2p). So rA - S = A(1-2p) = -A(2p-1). Thus, U-V = (rA - S)/D = -A(2p-1)/D.

So U-V = -A(2p-1)/D. Then O_x = - V (y+U v_y) / (y * (-A(2p-1)/D)) = V D (y+U v_y)/(y A(2p-1)). But V = S/D, so O_x = S (y+U v_y)/(y A(2p-1)). This is consistent with earlier.

Now, we can also compute O_y = (U v_x * V v_x - 0*V v_x) / Δ = U V v_x^2 / (v_x y (U-V)) = U V v_x / (y (U-V)). Then O_y = (U V v_x)/(y (U-V)). Using U-V = -A(2p-1)/D, we get O_y = U V v_x / (y * (-A(2p-1)/D)) = - U V v_x D/(y A(2p-1)). But U = rA/D, V = S/D, so U V = rA S / D^2. Then O_y = - (rA S / D^2) * v_x D/(y A(2p-1)) = - r S v_x/(y D (2p-1)). And v_x = (2p-1)A, so O_y = - r S A/(y D (2p-1)) * (2p-1)? Actually, v_x = (2p-1)A, so O_y = - r S (2p-1)A/(y D (2p-1)) = - r S A/(y D). So O_y = - r S A/(y D). That's a nice expression.

Now, also we have O_x = S (y+U v_y)/(y A(2p-1)). But we can compute y+U v_y in terms of p and r. We already have y = 2 sqrt(p(1-p)A(A-1)). And v_y = - (2A-1) sqrt(p(1-p)A)/ sqrt(A-1). Then O_x = S/(y A(2p-1)) * (y+U v_y) = S/( (2 sqrt(p(1-p)A(A-1))) A(2p-1) ) * sqrt(p(1-p)A) [2 sqrt(A-1) - rA (2A-1)/(D sqrt(A-1))] = S/(2 A(2p-1)) * (1/ sqrt(A-1)) * [2 sqrt(A-1) - rA (2A-1)/(D sqrt(A-1))] = S/(2 A(2p-1)) * [2 - rA (2A-1)/(D (A-1))]. So O_x = S/(A(2p-1)) - S r (2A-1)/(2 A D (A-1)). But we already have O_x = S/(A(2p-1)) - S r (2A-1)/(2 A D (A-1))? Wait, we had O_x = S/( (2p-1)A) - S r (2A-1)/(2 (2p-1) D (A-1))? Actually, from earlier: O_x = S/( (2p-1)A) - S r (2A-1)/(2 (2p-1) D (A-1))? Let's check: O_x = S/(A(2p-1)) - S r (2A-1)/(2 A D (A-1))? That would be if the second term had denominator 2 A D (A-1) but we have denominator 2 (2p-1) D (A-1) maybe. Let's derive carefully:

From O_x = S/(2 A(2p-1)) * [2 - rA (2A-1)/(D (A-1))] = S/(2 A(2p-1)) * 2 - S/(2 A(2p-1)) * rA (2A-1)/(D (A-1)) = S/(A(2p-1)) - S r (2A-1)/(2 (2p-1) D (A-1)). So O_x = S/(A(2p-1)) - S r (2A-1)/(2 (2p-1) D (A-1)). So that's the expression.

Multiply both sides by 2: 2S/(A(2p-1)) - S r (2A-1)/( (2p-1) D (A-1)) = 1.

Thus, S/(2p-1) [2/A - r (2A-1)/(D (A-1))] = 1.

Multiply both sides by A(2p-1): 2S - S r (2A-1)/Q = A(2p-1). So:

2S - A(2p-1) = S r (2A-1)/Q.

Thus, Q = S r (2A-1) / (2S - A(2p-1)).

Now, we can compute the right side. Since S = A(p+A-1), we have 2S - A(2p-1) = 2A(p+A-1) - A(2p-1) = A[2(p+A-1) - (2p-1)] = A[2p+2A-2 -2p+1] = A[2A -1] = A(2A-1). So 2S - A(2p-1) = A(2A-1). Then the right side becomes Q = S r (2A-1) / (A(2A-1)) = S r / A. So Q = S r / A.

But Q is defined as (2p-1)^2 A (A-1) + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p). So we need to check if (2p-1)^2 A (A-1) + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p) = S r / A.

Now, let's compute the left side. Since A = p+r, but we can treat p and A as variables. However, note that r = A-p, and p is between 1/2 and 1, A>1. So we can substitute A and p.

Now, compute the expression for A^2 - p^2 - A + p. Let's compute the left side and see if it simplifies to that.

Thus, N = A(2A-1) [ p(1-p)(1/2-p)/(A-1) - A(2p-1)/2 ].

Now, note that |v|^2 = (2p-1)^2 A^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)A/(A-1). So |v|^2 = A [ (2p-1)^2 A + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)/(A-1) ].

Now, we need to show that N^2 = |v|^2 * ((1/2-x)^2 + (y_O+y)^2). But we have expressions for (1/2-x) and (y_O+y). We already have (1/2-x) = (1/2-p) + A(2p-1). And we have y_O+y = (S - v_x/2)/v_y + y. We computed (S - v_x/2) + y v_y = A(2A-1)[1/2 - 2p(1-p)]. So y_O+y = A(2A-1)[1/2 - 2p(1-p)] / v_y. But v_y is negative, but that's fine. So (y_O+y)^2 = A^2 (2A-1)^2 [1/2 - 2p(1-p)]^2 / v_y^2.

Thus, ((1/2-x)^2 + (y_O+y)^2) = [ (1/2-p) + A(2p-1) ]^2 + A^2 (2A-1)^2 [1/2 - 2p(1-p)]^2 / v_y^2.

Now, multiply by |v|^2. Since |v|^2 = v_x^2+v_y^2, we have |v|^2 * ((1/2-x)^2 + (y_O+y)^2) = (v_x^2+v_y^2) * [ ((1/2-p)+A(2p-1))^2 + A^2 (2A-1)^2 [1/2 - 2p(1-p)]^2 / v_y^2 ] = (v_x^2+v_y^2) ((1/2-p)+A(2p-1))^2 + (v_x^2+v_y^2) A^2 (2A-1)^2 [1/2 - 2p(1-p)]^2 / v_y^2.

Now, N^2 = A^2 (2A-1)^2 [ p(1-p)(1/2-p)/(A-1) - A(2p-1)/2 ]^2.

We need to show that these are equal. This is a heavy algebraic identity that we have already seen in the context of O_x = 1/2. Possibly, we can also prove it by using the fact that O is the circumcenter, so the power of H with respect to the circle is something like (v·H - S)^2 / |v|^2? That is not directly N.

Thus, we have a rigorous algebraic proof that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Now, we need to present the proof in a clear, step-by-step manner. We can structure it as follows:

1. Set up coordinate system: Place M at (0,0), N at (1,0) after scaling so that distance MN=1 (since the problem is invariant under similarity). Let Ω: x^2+y^2=r^2, Γ: (x-1)^2+y^2=R^2, with r<R and the circles intersect in two points. Then A and B are symmetric about the x-axis; let A=(x,y) with y>0, then B=(x,-y). The intersection conditions give x = (1+r^2-R^2)/2, y = sqrt(r^2-x^2). Also, from the order C,M,N,D we have C=(-r,0), D=(1+R,0).

2. Compute the circumcenter P of triangle ACD. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = ( -r + (1+R) )/2 = (1+R-r)/2. Let p = (1+R-r)/2. Then the perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD) yields the y-coordinate of P: using PA=PC we derive q = -p(r+x)/y. So P = (p,q).

3. Orthocentre H of triangle PMN: M=(0,0), N=(1,0), P=(p,q). Since MN is horizontal, the altitude from P is vertical line x=p. The altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. Compute PN = (1-p, -q). Then the altitude from M has equation y = ((1-p)/q) x (since perpendicular slope = (1-p)/q). Intersection with x=p gives y_H = p(1-p)/q. Since q is negative, y_H negative. So H = (p, p(1-p)/q). But we can also express p(1-p)/q = - (1-p) y/(r+x) because q = -p(r+x)/y, so p(1-p)/q = p(1-p) * (-y/(p(r+x))) = -(1-p)y/(r+x). So H = (p, -(1-p)y/(r+x)). Also, note that r+x = 2(1-p)(p+r)?? Wait, we need to compute r+x in terms of p and r? Actually, we derived that r+x = 2(1-p)(p+r). This came from the intersection condition. So y_H = -y/(2(p+r)). This simplifies later.

4. Compute the line AP: direction v = P - A = (p-x, q-y). Also, we can compute t_E and t_F: The line AP meets Ω again at E, so E = A + t_E v with t_E = -2 (v·A)/|v|^2. Compute v·A = (p-x)x + (q-y)y = p x + q y - (x^2+y^2) = p x + q y - r^2. Using p x + q y = -p r (from earlier), we get v·A = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2. Similarly, for Γ, F = A + t_F v with t_F = -2 (v·(A-1))/|v|^2. Compute v·(A-1) = v·A - v·1 = -r(p+r) - (p-x) = -(p(p+r)+r^2? Actually, we will compute later; we get t_F = 2(p(1+r)+r^2-x)/|v|^2 = 2S/|v|^2, where S = p(1+r)+r^2-x.

5. Compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since B=(x,-y), and E,F are on line AP, we can use the fact that O satisfies |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2. By subtracting the equations, we obtain two linear equations in O. After solving, we find that O_x = 1/2. We can prove this by algebraic manipulation using the relations among parameters. One way: From |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, we get (O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - (x+t_E v_x))^2 + (O_y - (y+t_E v_y))^2. Expanding and simplifying gives: t_E v_x O_x + (2y + t_E v_y) O_y = t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) |v|^2. Since we have t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, and x v_x + y v_y = -r(p+r), we get the right side simplifies to 0. Thus, we have:

U v_x O_x + (y + U v_y) O_y = 0, where U = r(p+r)/D, with D=|v|^2.

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we get: V v_x O_x + (y + V v_y) O_y = V v_x, where V = S/D, and we have (S - v_x/2?) Actually, we need to show that the second equation simplifies to V v_x O_x + (y+V v_y) O_y = V v_x. Let's verify that: From |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we get:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - (x+t_F v_x))^2 + (O_y - (y+t_F v_y))^2 => Expand:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = -2(x+t_F v_x) O_x + (x+t_F v_x)^2 -2(y+t_F v_y) O_y + (y+t_F v_y)^2

Cancel x^2+y^2:

-2x O_x +2y O_y = -2x O_x -2t_F v_x O_x + 2x t_F v_x + t_F^2 v_x^2 -2y O_y -2t_F v_y O_y + 2y t_F v_y + t_F^2 v_y^2

Simplify: -2x O_x cancels? Actually, left has -2x O_x, right has -2x O_x -2t_F v_x O_x. So bring terms:

-2x O_x +2y O_y +2x O_x +2t_F v_x O_x +2t_F v_y O_y = 2x t_F v_x + t_F^2 v_x^2 + 2y t_F v_y + t_F^2 v_y^2

=> 2t_F v_x O_x + 2t_F v_y O_y = 2t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + t_F^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = 2t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + t_F^2 D.

Divide by 2: t_F v_x O_x + t_F v_y O_y = t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_F^2/2) D.

Now, note that x v_x + y v_y = x(p-x) + y(q-y) = p x + q y - (x^2+y^2) = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So t_F (x v_x + y v_y) = t_F (-r(p+r)) = -2S/D * r(p+r) = -2S r(p+r)/D. And (t_F^2/2) D = ( (4S^2/D^2)/2 ) D = 2S^2/D. So the right side becomes -2S r(p+r)/D + 2S^2/D = 2S(D? Actually, S^2 - S r(p+r)) = S(S - r(p+r)). Thus, right side = 2S v_x / D. So we have:

t_F v_x O_x + t_F v_y O_y = 2S v_x / D.

But t_F = 2S/D, so left side = (2S/D) v_x O_x + (2S/D) v_y O_y = (2S/D) (v_x O_x + v_y O_y). Thus, (2S/D) (v_x O_x + v_y O_y) = 2S v_x / D => v_x O_x + v_y O_y = v_x. So we get:

v_x O_x + v_y O_y = v_x.   (Eq2)

Similarly, from the first equation we derived: t_E v_x O_x + (2y + t_E v_y) O_y = 0, with t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, and we have from the first equation that t_E v_x O_x + (2y + t_E v_y) O_y = 0? U v_x O_x + (y+U v_y) O_y = 0.   (Eq1)

Now, we have two linear equations in O_x, O_y:

(1) U v_x O_x + (y+U v_y) O_y = 0.
(2) v_x O_x + v_y O_y = v_x.

We can solve for O_x. From (2), we have v_y O_y = v_x (1 - O_x). Given that we already have an exact algebraic verification that O_x = 1/2 for the numeric example, and we have a general derivation that O_x = 1/2 from the identity we proved earlier (the one that showed Δ = 0), we can present that as a lemma. We have the system:

(1) U v_x O_x + (y+U v_y) O_y = 0.
(2) v_x O_x + v_y O_y = v_x.

We want to solve for O_x. Using Cramer's rule:

Δ = (U v_x)(v_y) - (y+U v_y)(v_x) = U v_x v_y - v_x y - U v_x v_y = - v_x y. So Δ = - v_x y.

Then O_x = (0 * v_y - (y+U v_y) * v_x) / Δ = - (y+U v_y) v_x / (- v_x y) = (y+U v_y)/y = 1 + (U v_y)/y.

Thus, O_x = 1 + (U v_y)/y.

We have B=(0.38, -0.32496). O=(0.5, -0.3381). v=(0.22, -1.94976). t_E = 2r(p+r)/D = 2*0.5*1.1/3.8494 = 1.1/3.8494 = 0.2857. So E = A + t_E v = (0.38,0.32496)+0.2857*(0.22,-1.94976) = (0.38+0.06286=0.44286, 0.32496-0.5576=-0.2326). t_F = 2S/D = 2*0.77/3.8494 = 1.54/3.8494 = 0.4. So F = A + t_F v = (0.38+0.08785=0.46785, 0.32496-0.7799=-0.4549). Now, compute |O-B|^2: (0.5-0.38)^2+(-0.3381+0.32496)^2 = 0.12^2+(-0.01314)^2=0.0144+0.000173=0.014573. |O-E|^2: (0.5-0.44286)^2+(-0.3381+0.2326)^2 = (0.05714)^2+(-0.1055)^2=0.003264+0.01114=0.014404. These are not equal! So O is not the circumcenter of BEF? But we thought we derived O_x = 1/2 from the condition that the circle passes through B, E, F. Let's check if O=(0.5, -0.3381) satisfies |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2? Our calculation shows they are not equal. So maybe O is not (0.5, -0.3381). But earlier we solved for O_x and O_y from the linear system and got O_x=1/2, O_y = -11/(4√66) ≈ -0.3381. That O might not satisfy the equalities because we made a mistake in the linear equations. Let's re-derive the linear equations from |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 correctly.

We have B = (x, -y). E = (x + t_E v_x, y + t_E v_y). The condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 gives:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - (x+t_E v_x))^2 + (O_y - (y+t_E v_y))^2.

Expand both:

O_x^2 -2x O_x + x^2 + O_y^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = O_x^2 -2(x+t_E v_x)O_x + (x+t_E v_x)^2 + O_y^2 -2(y+t_E v_y)O_y + (y+t_E v_y)^2.

Cancel O_x^2 and O_y^2:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = -2(x+t_E v_x)O_x + (x^2+2x t_E v_x + t_E^2 v_x^2) -2(y+t_E v_y)O_y + (y^2+2y t_E v_y + t_E^2 v_y^2).

Simplify right side: -2x O_x -2t_E v_x O_x + x^2+2x t_E v_x + t_E^2 v_x^2 -2y O_y -2t_E v_y O_y + y^2+2y t_E v_y + t_E^2 v_y^2.

Now bring all terms to left:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 +2x O_x +2t_E v_x O_x - x^2 -2x t_E v_x - t_E^2 v_x^2 +2y O_y +2t_E v_y O_y - y^2 -2y t_E v_y - t_E^2 v_y^2 = 0.

Cancel x^2 and y^2: they appear with opposite signs. So:

(-2x O_x +2x O_x) = 0, so we get:

2t_E v_x O_x +2y O_y +2t_E v_y O_y -2x t_E v_x -2y t_E v_y - t_E^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = 0.

Thus:

2t_E v_x O_x + 2(t_E v_y + y) O_y - 2t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - t_E^2 D = 0.

Divide by 2:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0.

Now, note that x v_x + y v_y = x(p-x) + y(q-y) = p x + q y - (x^2+y^2) = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = t_E (-r(p+r)) = -t_E r(p+r). And t_E = 2r(p+r)/D. So t_E r(p+r) = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So the equation becomes:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y + t_E r(p+r) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0.

Now, t_E r(p+r) = (2r(p+r)/D) * r(p+r) = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. And (t_E^2/2) D = ( (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 ) D = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So these two terms cancel! Indeed, t_E r(p+r) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0. So we are left with:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.

Thus, we have:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.   (Eq1')

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we get:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = 0.   (Eq2')

But wait, we also have the second equation from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2? Let's derive that. The derivation is analogous, with t_F instead of t_E. So we get:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = 0.   (Eq2')

So we have two linear equations in O_x, O_y:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.
t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = 0.

But these are two homogeneous equations; they are not independent generally. In fact, they both give a relation that O must satisfy. For the circumcenter, we also have that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of BE, which is the same as the condition that O is equidistant from B and E, which we already used. So these two equations are both derived from that condition, but they are not two independent conditions; they are the same condition for different points? Actually, we have two equations because we have two equalities: |O-B|=|O-E| and |O-B|=|O-F|. These are two conditions, and they give two equations that O must satisfy. They are not both homogeneous; they are both linear equations that involve O. But we derived that after simplification, each gives:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.
t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = 0.

These are two linear equations in O_x, O_y. They are not homogeneous in the sense that the right side is 0, but they are both linear equations with no constant term. So they represent two lines through the origin. For a non-trivial solution O, these two lines must be the same, i.e., the coefficients are proportional. That gives a condition that we must have:

(t_E v_x) / (t_F v_x) = (t_E v_y + y) / (t_F v_y + y). That is, the two equations are equivalent. So O must satisfy one of them. But we also need that O is the circumcenter, so we need to find O that satisfies both? Actually, if the two equations are equivalent, then any O that satisfies one automatically satisfies the other. So we have a single linear equation. But we also need that O is equidistant from B and E and from B and F, which are two conditions; they should determine O uniquely (since the perpendicular bisector of BE and BF intersect at O). So there should be two independent linear equations. But we derived that each equality simplifies to a linear equation that is homogeneous? Let's check the derivation again carefully. We had:

From |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, we got:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.

That seems to be correct after cancellation. But wait, we also had the term -2x t_E v_x? Let's re-derive from the start with the correct algebra.

We have:

|O-B|^2 = (O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2.
|O-E|^2 = (O_x - (x+t_E v_x))^2 + (O_y - (y+t_E v_y))^2.

Set equal:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - x - t_E v_x)^2 + (O_y - y - t_E v_y)^2.

Expand:

O_x^2 -2x O_x + x^2 + O_y^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = O_x^2 -2(x+t_E v_x)O_x + (x+t_E v_x)^2 + O_y^2 -2(y+t_E v_y)O_y + (y+t_E v_y)^2.

Cancel O_x^2, O_y^2:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = -2x O_x -2t_E v_x O_x + x^2+2x t_E v_x + t_E^2 v_x^2 + -2y O_y -2t_E v_y O_y + y^2+2y t_E v_y + t_E^2 v_y^2.

Bring all terms to left:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 +2x O_x +2t_E v_x O_x - x^2 -2x t_E v_x - t_E^2 v_x^2 +2y O_y +2t_E v_y O_y - y^2 -2y t_E v_y - t_E^2 v_y^2 = 0.

Now, combine like terms:

(-2x O_x +2x O_x) = 0.
x^2 - x^2 = 0.
y^2 - y^2 = 0.
So we are left with:

2t_E v_x O_x -2x t_E v_x +2y O_y +2t_E v_y O_y -2y t_E v_y - t_E^2(v_x^2+v_y^2) = 0.

Thus:

2t_E v_x O_x + 2(t_E v_y + y) O_y - 2t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - t_E^2 D = 0.

Divide by 2:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0.

So that is the correct equation.

Now, we need to compute x v_x + y v_y. We have v_x = p-x, v_y = q-y. So x v_x + y v_y = x(p-x) + y(q-y) = p x - x^2 + q y - y^2 = (p x + q y) - (x^2+y^2) = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So that is correct.

Then t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = t_E * (-r(p+r)) = -t_E r(p+r). And (t_E^2/2) D = ( (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 ) D = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So the equation becomes:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y - (-t_E r(p+r)) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0? Wait, careful: The term is - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = - ( -t_E r(p+r) ) - (t_E^2/2) D = t_E r(p+r) - (t_E^2/2) D. So the equation is:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y + t_E r(p+r) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0.

Now, t_E r(p+r) = (2r(p+r)/D) * r(p+r) = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. And (t_E^2/2) D = ( (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 ) D = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So they cancel! So indeed, the equation simplifies to:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.   (Eq1')

Thus, the equality |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 gives a linear equation with no constant term. Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we get:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = 0.   (Eq2')

So we have two homogeneous linear equations in O_x, O_y. For a non-trivial O (which is not the zero vector because O is a point), these two equations must be dependent, i.e., the coefficient vectors are proportional. That is, there exists a constant k such that:

t_E v_x = k t_F v_x and t_E v_y + y = k (t_F v_y + y).

Since v_x is not zero (p>1/2, A>1, so v_x>0), we have k = t_E/t_F. So we get:

t_E v_y + y = (t_E/t_F) (t_F v_y + y) = t_E v_y + (t_E/t_F) y.

Thus, we have:

t_E v_y + y = t_E v_y + (t_E/t_F) y => y = (t_E/t_F) y => (1 - t_E/t_F) y = 0 => t_E = t_F, which is not generally true. So there is no non-trivial O that satisfies both equations? That would imply that the circumcenter O does not exist? That can't be. So where is the error? We must have made a mistake in the derivation of the second equation. Perhaps the second equation from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2 is not homogeneous? Let's derive it carefully.

We have B = (x, -y). F = (x + t_F v_x, y + t_F v_y). The condition |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2 gives:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - x - t_F v_x)^2 + (O_y - y - t_F v_y)^2.

Expanding as before:

O_x^2 -2x O_x + x^2 + O_y^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = O_x^2 -2(x+t_F v_x)O_x + (x+t_F v_x)^2 + O_y^2 -2(y+t_F v_y)O_y + (y+t_F v_y)^2.

Cancel O_x^2, O_y^2:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 = -2x O_x -2t_F v_x O_x + x^2+2x t_F v_x + t_F^2 v_x^2 -2y O_y -2t_F v_y O_y + y^2+2y t_F v_y + t_F^2 v_y^2.

Bring all to left:

-2x O_x + x^2 +2y O_y + y^2 +2x O_x +2t_F v_x O_x - x^2 -2x t_F v_x - t_F^2 v_x^2 +2y O_y +2t_F v_y O_y - y^2 -2y t_F v_y - t_F^2 v_y^2 = 0.

Cancel x^2, y^2, and -2x O_x+2x O_x=0. So:

2t_F v_x O_x + 2y O_y +2t_F v_y O_y -2x t_F v_x -2y t_F v_y - t_F^2 D = 0.

Thus:

2t_F v_x O_x + 2(t_F v_y + y) O_y - 2t_F (x v_x + y v_y) - t_F^2 D = 0.

Divide by 2:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y - t_F (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_F^2/2) D = 0.

Now, x v_x + y v_y = -r(p+r). So - t_F (x v_x + y v_y) = -t_F * (-r(p+r)) = t_F r(p+r). And (t_F^2/2) D =? t_F = 2S/D, where S = p(1+r)+r^2-x. So t_F r(p+r) = (2S/D) r(p+r) = 2S r(p+r)/D. And (t_F^2/2) D = ( (4S^2/D^2)/2 ) D = 2S^2/D. So the equation becomes:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y + 2S r(p+r)/D - 2S^2/D = 0.

But we do not have cancellation in general; the term 2S r(p+r)/D - 2S^2/D = 2S( r(p+r) - S)/D. And we earlier computed that r(p+r) - S =? We have S = p(1+r)+r^2-x. Also, r(p+r) = rp + r^2. So r(p+r) - S = rp+r^2 - (p+pr+r^2 - x) = rp+r^2 - p - pr - r^2 + x = x - p = -(p-x) = -v_x. So r(p+r) - S = -v_x. Thus, 2S( r(p+r) - S)/D = 2S(-v_x)/D = -2S v_x/D = -2S v_x/D. But note that t_F v_x = (2S/D) v_x = 2S v_x/D. So the constant term is -t_F v_x. So the equation becomes:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y - t_F v_x = 0.

Thus, we have:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = t_F v_x.   (Eq2')

So the second equation is not homogeneous; it has a constant term. So we have:

Eq1': t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.
Eq2': t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = t_F v_x.

Now, these are two linear equations in O_x, O_y. We can solve them. Let's do that.

Write them as:

(1) t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.
(2) t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = t_F v_x.

We can solve for O_x and O_y. Let's denote:

A = t_E v_x, B = t_E v_y + y.
C = t_F v_x, D = t_F v_y + y.
Right side: R = t_F v_x.

Then the system is:

A O_x + B O_y = 0.
C O_x + D O_y = R.

Solve using Cramer's rule:

Δ = A D - B C = t_E v_x (t_F v_y + y) - (t_E v_y + y) t_F v_x = t_E v_x t_F v_y + t_E v_x y - t_E v_y t_F v_x - y t_F v_x = t_E v_x y - y t_F v_x = y v_x (t_E - t_F).

Thus, Δ = y v_x (t_E - t_F). Since v_x>0, y>0, t_E ≠ t_F generally. So Δ ≠ 0.

Now, O_x = (0 * D - B * R) / Δ = - B R / Δ = - (t_E v_y + y) * t_F v_x / (y v_x (t_E - t_F)) = - (t_E v_y + y) t_F / (y (t_E - t_F)).

And O_y = (A * R - 0 * C) / Δ = A R / Δ = t_E v_x * t_F v_x / (y v_x (t_E - t_F)) = t_E t_F v_x / (y (t_E - t_F)).

Thus, O_x = - t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)). O_y = t_E t_F v_x / (y (t_E - t_F)).

Now, we want to show that O_x = 1/2. So we need to prove that:

- t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)) = 1/2 => Multiply both sides by 2y(t_E-t_F): -2 t_F (t_E v_y + y) = y (t_E - t_F). So:

-2 t_F t_E v_y - 2 t_F y = y t_E - y t_F => Bring terms: -2 t_F t_E v_y - 2 t_F y - y t_E + y t_F = 0 => -2 t_F t_E v_y - y t_E - y t_F = 0? Because -2 t_F y + y t_F = - y t_F. So:

-2 t_F t_E v_y - y t_E - y t_F = 0 => Multiply by -1: 2 t_F t_E v_y + y (t_E + t_F) = 0 => 2 t_E t_F v_y + y (t_E + t_F) = 0.

Thus, we need to prove that 2 t_E t_F v_y + y (t_E + t_F) = 0.

Now, we have t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, t_F = 2S/D, and v_y = q-y. So we can substitute. This is an algebraic identity that should hold given the relations. Let's test with numeric: t_E = 0.2857, t_F = 0.4, v_y = -1.94976, so 2 t_E t_F v_y = 2*0.2857*0.4*(-1.94976) = 2*0.11428*(-1.94976) = 0.22856*(-1.94976) = -0.445. y (t_E+t_F)=0.32496*(0.2857+0.4)=0.32496*0.6857=0.2225. Sum = -0.445+0.2225 = -0.2225, not zero. So the sum is not zero. But our O_x from this formula should give O_x = 1/2? Let's compute O_x using these values: O_x = - t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)). t_E v_y = 0.2857*(-1.94976)= -0.557, t_E v_y + y = -0.557+0.32496= -0.23204. Then -t_F times that = -0.4*(-0.23204)=0.092816. Denominator: y(t_E-t_F)=0.32496*(0.2857-0.4)=0.32496*(-0.1143)= -0.03713. So O_x = 0.092816/(-0.03713)= -2.5. That's not 0.5. So the system we solved is not correct? But we derived Eq1' and Eq2' from the equalities. Let's test if O=(0.5, -0.3381) satisfies Eq1' and Eq2'. For O=(0.5, -0.3381), compute t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.2857*0.22*0.5 + (-0.557+0.32496)*(-0.3381) = 0.2857*0.11 + (-0.23204)*(-0.3381) = 0.03143 + 0.0784 = 0.1098, not 0. So O does not satisfy Eq1'. That means our derived Eq1' is not correct? Let's recalc the derivation of Eq1' step by step with the numeric values to see if we made an algebra error.

We had: from |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, we got:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0.

And we claimed that - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0? Let's compute that numerically.

But we thought we had computed O from the system, but maybe we made a mistake in solving for O from the linear system? Let's directly compute the circumcenter of B, E, F for the numeric example to see if O=(0.5, -0.3381) is indeed the circumcenter. We earlier solved the linear system for O and got O_x=1/2, O_y = -11/(4√66) ≈ -0.3381. But we need to verify if that O is equidistant from B, E, F. Let's compute the circumcenter of BEF directly using coordinates to see what it is.

We have B=(0.38, -0.32496), E=(0.44286, -0.2326), F=(0.46785, -0.4549). Compute the perpendicular bisector of BE and BF. Use numeric.

Midpoint of BE: M_BE = ((0.38+0.44286)/2, (-0.32496-0.2326)/2) = (0.41143, -0.27878). Slope of BE: ( -0.2326 - (-0.32496) )/(0.44286-0.38) = (0.09236)/(0.06286)=1.468. So perpendicular slope = -1/1.468 = -0.681. Equation of perpendicular bisector of BE: y + 0.27878 = -0.681 (x - 0.41143). Now, we need to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). We have O=(1/2, y_O) with y_O = -√66/24, and we have H and v.

Let's compute H for numeric exactly: H = (p, y_H) with p=3/5, y_H = -y/(2A) = -(√66/25) / (22/10) = -(√66/25) * (10/22) = -(√66/25)*(5/11)= -5√66/(275)= -√66/55. So H = (3/5, -√66/55). v = (11/50, -6√66/25). So the line L through H parallel to v: param L = H + t v. We want to show that distance from O to L equals radius OB.

We already have O=(1/2, -√66/24). Compute the distance from O to L. The distance from point O to line through H with direction v is |(O-H) × v| / |v|. Compute O-H = (1/2 - 3/5, -√66/24 + √66/55) = (5/10 - 6/10, √66(-1/24+1/55)) = (-1/10, √66(-55/1320+24/1320)) = (-1/10, √66(-31/1320)) = (-1/10, -31√66/1320). Therefore, the distance from O to L equals OB, i.e., the radius. Hence, L is tangent to circle (BEF). This is proven exactly using fractions.

Thus, we have a rigorous proof.

Now, we need to present the proof in a clear, step-by-step manner. We can do it as follows:

- Set up coordinate system with M=(0,0), N=(1,0) after scaling.
- Let Ω: x^2+y^2=r^2, Γ: (x-1)^2+y^2=R^2, with r<R and circles intersect.
- Determine coordinates: A=(x,y) with y>0, x=(1+r^2-R^2)/2, y=√(r^2-x^2). B=(x,-y).
- Compute P: circumcenter of ACD. Since C=(-r,0), D=(1+R,0). The perpendicular bisector of CD is x=p where p=(1+R-r)/2. Using PA=PC we get q = -p(r+x)/y. So P=(p,q).
- Compute H: orthocenter of PMN. M=(0,0), N=(1,0). Altitude from P is vertical line x=p. Altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. PN=(1-p,-q). So altitude from M: y = ((1-p)/q)x. Intersection with x=p gives y_H = p(1-p)/q. Using q = -p(r+x)/y, we get y_H = -y/(2(p+r))? Actually, we derived earlier y_H = -y/(2A) with A=p+r, and also y_H = - (1-p) y/(r+x). But we can also directly compute y_H = -y/(2(p+r)) from the intersection condition. Let's derive that: Since r+x = 2(1-p)(p+r), we have y_H = - (1-p) y/(r+x) = - (1-p) y / (2(1-p)(p+r)) = - y/(2(p+r)). So H = (p, -y/(2(p+r))). This is a nice simplification.

- Compute the line AP: direction v = (p-x, q-y). Also, compute t_E and t_F: The line AP meets Ω again at E, so E = A + t_E v, where t_E = -2 (v·A)/|v|^2. Compute v·A = (p-x)x+(q-y)y = p x + q y - (x^2+y^2) = p x + q y - r^2. Since p x + q y = -p r (from earlier: p x + q y = -p r? Let's check: p x + q y =? We have from P: p x + q y = -p r? Actually, we derived that from PA=PC: p x + q y = -p r. Let's verify: p x + q y = p x + (-p(r+x)/y) y = p x - p(r+x) = p x - p r - p x = -p r. So yes, p x + q y = -p r. Then v·A = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, where D=|v|^2.
- Similarly, for Γ, F = A + t_F v, with t_F = -2 (v·(A-1))/D. Compute v·(A-1) = v·A - v·1 = -r(p+r) - (p-x) = -(p-x) - r(p+r) = -(p-x) - r(p+r). Then -2 times that divided by D gives t_F = 2(p(1+r)+r^2-x)/D? Actually, we can derive that t_F = 2S/D where S = p(1+r)+r^2-x. We'll keep that.

- Now, find circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Use the equalities |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 and |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2. After algebraic simplification, we obtain the linear system:

   (i) 11 X + 2√66 Y = 0? That was specific to the numeric example; we need a general expression.

We need to do the general simplification. Let's do it in general.

We have B=(x, -y). E=(x + t_E v_x, y + t_E v_y). F=(x + t_F v_x, y + t_F v_y). And we have v_x = p-x, v_y = q-y. Also, we have t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, t_F = 2S/D, where D = v_x^2+v_y^2, and S = p(1+r)+r^2-x.

Now, the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 gives:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - x - t_E v_x)^2 + (O_y - y - t_E v_y)^2.

Expanding and simplifying, we get:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) D.

But x v_x + y v_y =? We have x v_x + y v_y = x(p-x) + y(q-y) = p x - x^2 + q y - y^2 = (p x + q y) - (x^2+y^2) = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = t_E * (-r(p+r)) = -t_E r(p+r). And (t_E^2/2) D = (t_E^2/2) D. But note that t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, so t_E r(p+r) = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D, and (t_E^2/2) D = ( (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 ) D = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So these two terms cancel! Therefore, the equation reduces to:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.   (1)

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we get:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_F^2/2) D.

Now, x v_x + y v_y = -r(p+r). So t_F (x v_x + y v_y) = -t_F r(p+r). And (t_F^2/2) D =? t_F = 2S/D, so t_F r(p+r) = 2S r(p+r)/D, and (t_F^2/2) D = 2S^2/D. So the constant term is -2S r(p+r)/D + 2S^2/D = 2S( r(p+r) - S )/D. But we computed earlier that r(p+r) - S = -v_x. So the constant term = -2S v_x/D = -t_F v_x. Thus, the equation becomes:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = t_F v_x.   (2)

So we have the system:

(1) t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.
(2) t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + y) O_y = t_F v_x.

Now, we can solve for O_x and O_y. Let's do it in general.

Let’s denote:

a = t_E v_x, b = t_E v_y + y.
c = t_F v_x, d = t_F v_y + y.
RHS = t_F v_x = c.

Then the system is:

a O_x + b O_y = 0.
c O_x + d O_y = c.

The determinant Δ = a d - b c = t_E v_x (t_F v_y + y) - (t_E v_y + y) t_F v_x = t_E v_x t_F v_y + t_E v_x y - t_E v_y t_F v_x - y t_F v_x = t_E v_x y - y t_F v_x = y v_x (t_E - t_F). So Δ = y v_x (t_E - t_F).

Now, compute O_x = (0 * d - b * c) / Δ = -b c / Δ = - (t_E v_y + y) * t_F v_x / (y v_x (t_E - t_F)) = - t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)). Similarly, O_y = (a * c - 0 * c) / Δ = a c / Δ = t_E v_x * t_F v_x / (y v_x (t_E - t_F)) = t_E t_F v_x / (y (t_E - t_F)).

Thus, O_x = - t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)). O_y = t_E t_F v_x / (y (t_E - t_F)).

Now, we want to show that O_x = 1/2. So we need to prove:

- t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)) = 1/2.

Multiply both sides by 2y(t_E - t_F): -2 t_F (t_E v_y + y) = y (t_E - t_F). Rearranged: 2 t_F (t_E v_y + y) + y (t_E - t_F) = 0 => 2 t_F t_E v_y + 2 t_F y + y t_E - y t_F = 0 => 2 t_E t_F v_y + y (t_E + t_F) = 0.

So we need to prove:

2 t_E t_F v_y + y (t_E + t_F) = 0.   (3)

Now, we have t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, t_F = 2S/D, and v_y = q-y. So substitute:

2 * (2r(p+r)/D) * (2S/D) * v_y + y (2r(p+r)/D + 2S/D) = 0 => (8 r S v_y)/D^2 + (2y (r(p+r)+S)/D) = 0 => Multiply by D^2: 8 r S v_y + 2y (r(p+r)+S) D = 0 => Divide by 2: 4 r S v_y + y (r(p+r)+S) D = 0.   (4)

Now, we need to show that this holds given the relations among parameters. We can compute D = v_x^2+v_y^2, and we have expressions for v_x, v_y, r, S, etc., in terms of p and A = p+r. Let's do that.

Recall: A = p+r. Then r = A-p. Also, from the intersection condition, we have:

y = 2 √(p(1-p)A(A-1))? Actually, we derived that y^2 = 4p(1-p)(A-1)A. So y = 2 √(p(1-p)A(A-1)). And v_x = (2p-1)A. v_y = - (2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1). Also, S = p(1+r)+r^2 - x = p + pr + r^2 - x. But we also have x = r - 2p(A-1) = (A-p) - 2p(A-1) = A-p -2pA+2p = A(1-2p) + p. So S = p + p(A-p) + (A-p)^2 - (A(1-2p)+p) = p + pA - p^2 + A^2 -2Ap + p^2 - A +2pA - p = simplify: p cancels? Let's compute: p + pA - p^2 + A^2 -2Ap + p^2 - A +2pA - p = combine p terms: p - p = 0. pA terms: pA -2Ap +2pA = pA. So we have pA + A^2 - A = A(p+A-1). So S = A(p+A-1). And also r(p+r) = (A-p)A = A(A-p). So r(p+r)+S = A(A-p) + A(p+A-1) = A(A-p+p+A-1) = A(2A-1). So r(p+r)+S = A(2A-1). And also rS = (A-p)A * A(p+A-1) = A^2 (A-p)(p+A-1). And v_y = - (2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1). So the left side of (4) is:

4 r S v_y = 4 * A^2 (A-p)(p+A-1) * [ - (2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1) ] = -4 A^2 (A-p)(p+A-1)(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1).

And y (r(p+r)+S) D = y * A(2A-1) * D = 2 √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) * A(2A-1) * D = 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) D.

So the equation (4) becomes:

-4 A^2 (A-p)(p+A-1)(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1) + 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) D = 0.

Divide both sides by 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) (which is positive, since A>0, 2A-1>0, √(p(1-p)A)>0). We get:

-2 A (A-p)(p+A-1)/√(A-1) + √(A-1) D = 0 => Multiply by √(A-1): -2 A (A-p)(p+A-1) + (A-1) D = 0 => So (A-1) D = 2 A (A-p)(p+A-1).   (5)

Now, we need to check if this identity holds given that D = v_x^2+v_y^2 = (2p-1)^2 A^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)A/(A-1). So compute (A-1) D = (A-1)[ (2p-1)^2 A^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)A/(A-1) ] = (A-1)(2p-1)^2 A^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)A.

Thus, (5) becomes:

(A-1)(2p-1)^2 A^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)A = 2 A (A-p)(p+A-1).

Divide both sides by A (A>0): (A-1)(2p-1)^2 A + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p) = 2 (A-p)(p+A-1).

Now, this is an equation that must be true for all p and A that satisfy the intersection condition? But note that A = p+r, and r is positive, so A > p. Also, p is between 1/2 and 1, and A>1. We can test if this is an identity. Let's expand both sides.

Left side: (A-1)(4p^2-4p+1)A + (4A^2-4A+1)p(1-p) = A(A-1)(4p^2-4p+1) + p(1-p)(4A^2-4A+1).

Right side: 2(A-p)(p+A-1) = 2(A-p)(A+p-1) = 2[(A-p)(A+p-1)] = 2[(A^2 - p^2 - A + p)] = 2A^2 - 2p^2 - 2A + 2p.

Now, we can expand the left side:

First term: A(A-1)(4p^2-4p+1) = (A^2 - A)(4p^2-4p+1) = (4p^2-4p+1)A^2 - (4p^2-4p+1)A.
Second term: p(1-p)(4A^2-4A+1) = (p-p^2)(4A^2-4A+1) = 4pA^2 -4p^2A^2 + p - p^2? Actually, expand: (p-p^2)*(4A^2-4A+1) = p*(4A^2-4A+1) - p^2*(4A^2-4A+1) = 4pA^2 -4pA + p -4p^2A^2 +4p^2A - p^2.

So left side sum = (4p^2-4p+1)A^2 - (4p^2-4p+1)A + 4pA^2 -4p^2A^2 -4pA + p -4p^2A^2 +4p^2A - p^2? Wait, careful: The second term has -4p^2A^2? Actually, we have from second term: 4pA^2 -4p^2A^2? Let's combine correctly.

Left side = (4p^2-4p+1)A^2 - (4p^2-4p+1)A + 4pA^2 -4p^2A^2? No, the second term's A^2 coefficient is from p*(4A^2) = 4pA^2, and from -p^2*(4A^2) = -4p^2A^2. So total A^2 coefficient: (4p^2-4p+1) + 4p -4p^2 = (4p^2-4p+1+4p-4p^2) = 1. So left side = A^2 + (coefficient for A terms) + constant.

Now, A terms: from first term: - (4p^2-4p+1)A. From second term: -4pA +4p^2A? Actually, second term's A coefficient: from p*(-4A) = -4pA, and from -p^2*(-4A) = +4p^2A? Wait, second term expansion: (p-p^2)*(4A^2-4A+1) = p*4A^2 - p*4A + p*1 - p^2*4A^2 + p^2*4A - p^2*1 = 4pA^2 -4pA + p -4p^2A^2 +4p^2A - p^2. So A coefficient: -4p + 4p^2. So total A coefficient: -(4p^2-4p+1) + (-4p+4p^2) = -4p^2+4p-1 -4p+4p^2 = -1. So A term = -A.

Constant terms: from second term: p - p^2. So left side = A^2 - A + p - p^2.

Thus, the equation (5) becomes:

A^2 - A + p - p^2 = 2(A-p)(A+p-1) = 2(A^2 - p^2 - A + p) = 2A^2 - 2p^2 - 2A + 2p.

Now, bring all to one side: 0 = 2A^2 - 2p^2 - 2A + 2p - (A^2 - A + p - p^2) = A^2 - 2p^2 - 2A + 2p + A - p + p^2 = A^2 - p^2 - A + p = (A-p)(A+p-1). So the equation reduces to (A-p)(A+p-1)=0. Since A>p and A>1, we have A-p>0 and A+p-1>0 (since A>1, p>1/2, so A+p-1>0). Thus, (A-p)(A+p-1) ≠ 0. So the equation is not an identity; it's false. But we have derived that (5) must hold for the circumcenter to satisfy O_x=1/2. However, we know from the numeric example that O_x=1/2 holds. So there must be an error in the derivation of the condition (5). Let's trace back.

We had equation (4): 4 r S v_y + y (r(p+r)+S) D = 0. We substituted the expressions and got:

-4 A^2 (A-p)(p+A-1)(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1) + 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) D = 0.

Then we divided by 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) to get:

-2 A (A-p)(p+A-1)/√(A-1) + √(A-1) D = 0.

Then we multiplied by √(A-1) to get:

-2 A (A-p)(p+A-1) + (A-1) D = 0.

But note that √(A-1) D =? We have D = v_x^2+v_y^2. And we have √(A-1) D. But earlier we had √(A-1) D =? Actually, the term we had was: 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) D? Wait, let's recompute the expression after division more carefully.

We have:

Term1: 4 r S v_y. r = A-p. S = A(p+A-1). v_y = - (2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1). So:

4 r S v_y = 4 (A-p) * A(p+A-1) * [ - (2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1) ] = -4 A (A-p)(p+A-1)(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1).

Term2: y (r(p+r)+S) D. y = 2 √(p(1-p)A(A-1)). r(p+r)+S = A(2A-1) (since we derived r(p+r)+S = A(2A-1)). So:

y (r(p+r)+S) D = 2 √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) * A(2A-1) * D = 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) D.

Thus, equation (4) is:

-4 A (A-p)(p+A-1)(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1) + 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) D = 0.

Factor out 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) (which is positive). Then we get:

2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) [ -2 (A-p)(p+A-1)/√(A-1) + √(A-1) D ] = 0? Actually, note that the first term has a factor 4 A (A-p)(p+A-1)(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1) = 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) * [2 (A-p)(p+A-1)/√(A-1) ]. Wait, let's factor:

First term: -4 A (A-p)(p+A-1)(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1) = 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) * [ -2 (A-p)(p+A-1)/√(A-1) ].

Second term: 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A(A-1)) D = 2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) * [ √(A-1) D ].

Thus, the equation becomes:

2A(2A-1) √(p(1-p)A) [ -2 (A-p)(p+A-1)/√(A-1) + √(A-1) D ] = 0.

Since the factor is positive, we have:

-2 (A-p)(p+A-1)/√(A-1) + √(A-1) D = 0 => Multiply by √(A-1): -2 (A-p)(p+A-1) + (A-1) D = 0 => (A-1) D = 2 (A-p)(p+A-1). So that's (5). So the algebra is correct.

Now, we need to check if this equation holds given the expression for D. Let's compute (A-1) D using the expressions for v_x and v_y. We have:

v_x = (2p-1)A.
v_y = - (2A-1) √(p(1-p)A)/√(A-1).

So D = (2p-1)^2 A^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)A/(A-1) = A[ (2p-1)^2 A + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)/(A-1) ].

Thus, (A-1) D = (A-1) * A[ (2p-1)^2 A + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p)/(A-1) ] = A(A-1)(2p-1)^2 A + A(2A-1)^2 p(1-p) = A^2 (A-1)(2p-1)^2 + A(2A-1)^2 p(1-p). So (5) becomes:

A^2 (A-1)(2p-1)^2 + A(2A-1)^2 p(1-p) = 2 A (A-p)(p+A-1).

Divide by A: A (A-1)(2p-1)^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p) = 2 (A-p)(p+A-1). Now, this is an equation that must hold. But we earlier expanded the left side and got A^2 - A + p - p^2? Let's recompute the left side expansion correctly.

Let L = A (A-1)(2p-1)^2 + (2A-1)^2 p(1-p). Compute (2p-1)^2 = 4p^2-4p+1. So first term: A(A-1)(4p^2-4p+1) = (A^2-A)(4p^2-4p+1) = (4p^2-4p+1)A^2 - (4p^2-4p+1)A.
Second term: (2A-1)^2 p(1-p) = (4A^2-4A+1)p(1-p) = p(1-p)(4A^2-4A+1) = (p-p^2)(4A^2-4A+1) = 4pA^2 -4p^2A^2 -4pA +4p^2A + p - p^2.
So L = (4p^2-4p+1)A^2 - (4p^2-4p+1)A + 4pA^2 -4p^2A^2 -4pA +4p^2A + p - p^2.
Combine A^2 terms: A^2[(4p^2-4p+1) + 4p -4p^2] = A^2[1] = A^2.
Combine A terms: A[ - (4p^2-4p+1) -4p +4p^2 ] = A[ -4p^2+4p-1 -4p +4p^2 ] = A[-1] = -A.
Constant: p - p^2.
So L = A^2 - A + p - p^2.
Thus, the equation becomes:

A^2 - A + p - p^2 = 2 (A-p)(p+A-1) = 2(A-p)(A+p-1) = 2(A^2 - p^2 - A + p) = 2A^2 - 2p^2 - 2A + 2p.

Now, bring all to one side: 0 = 2A^2 - 2p^2 - 2A + 2p - (A^2 - A + p - p^2) = A^2 - p^2 - A + p = (A-p)(A+p-1). So we get (A-p)(A+p-1)=0. Since A>p and A>1, this is false. Therefore, the equation (5) is not satisfied. But we know from the numeric example that O_x=1/2 holds. So there must be a mistake in the derivation of the condition (3) from O_x = 1/2. Let's re-derive the condition (3) from O_x = 1/2.

We have O_x = - t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)). We set O_x = 1/2. So:

- t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)) = 1/2 => Multiply both sides by 2y(t_E - t_F): -2 t_F (t_E v_y + y) = y (t_E - t_F). Rearranged: 2 t_F (t_E v_y + y) + y (t_E - t_F) = 0 => 2 t_F t_E v_y + 2 t_F y + y t_E - y t_F = 0 => 2 t_E t_F v_y + y (t_E + t_F) = 0. So that is correct.

Now, we need to check if this holds for the numeric example. Compute t_E=2/7, t_F=2/5, v_y = -6√66/25? Actually, v_y = -6√66/25, but we have exact values. Let's compute 2 t_E t_F v_y = 2*(2/7)*(2/5)*v_y = (8/35)*v_y = (8/35)*(-6√66/25) = -48√66/(875). y (t_E+t_F) = (√66/25)*(2/7+2/5)= (√66/25)*((10+14)/35)= (√66/25)*(24/35)= 24√66/(875). So sum = -48√66/875 + 24√66/875 = -24√66/875, not zero. So the equation is not satisfied. But we have already computed O_x from the linear system and got O_x=1/2 exactly. So our derived expression for O_x must be incorrect for the numeric example. Let's compute O_x using the formula O_x = - t_F (t_E v_y + y) / (y (t_E - t_F)) with the numeric exact values.

t_E = 2/7, t_F = 2/5. v_y = -6√66/25. y = √66/25. Compute t_E v_y = (2/7)*(-6√66/25)= -12√66/(175). Then t_E v_y + y = -12√66/175 + √66/25 = -12√66/175 + 7√66/175 = -5√66/175 = -√66/35. Then -t_F (t_E v_y + y) = -(2/5)*(-√66/35)= (2/5)*(√66/35)= 2√66/(175). Denominator: y(t_E-t_F)= (√66/25)*(2/7-2/5)= (√66/25)*((10-14)/35)= (√66/25)*(-4/35)= -4√66/(875). So O_x = (2√66/175) / ( -4√66/875 ) = (2/175) * ( -875/4 ) = - (2*875)/(175*4) = - (2*5)/4 = -10/4 = -2.5. That gives O_x = -2.5, not 0.5. So the formula for O_x from the linear system is not correct? But we derived it from the system (1) and (2). Let's check if the system (1) and (2) is correct for the numeric example. We had Eq1': t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0. For O=(1/2, -√66/24), compute t_E v_x = (2/7)*(11/50)=22/350=11/175. So t_E v_x O_x = (11/175)*(1/2)=11/350. t_E v_y + y = -5√66/175? Actually, we computed t_E v_y + y = -√66/35 = -5√66/175? Because √66/35 = 5√66/175. So t_E v_y + y = -5√66/175. Then O_y = -√66/24, so product = (-5√66/175)*(-√66/24)=5*66/(175*24)=330/(4200)=33/420=11/140? Actually, 330/4200=33/420=11/140. So sum = 11/350 + 11/140 = 11/350 + 11/140 = common denominator 700: 11*2/700=22/700, 11*5/700=55/700, total 77/700=11/100=0.11. Not 0. So O does not satisfy Eq1'. But we derived Eq1' from the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2. Let's check if O satisfies that condition numerically. We computed |O-B|^2 = 7/480 ≈0.014583, |O-E|^2 =? Using O=(1/2, -√66/24), E=(31/70, -√66/35). Compute differences: dx=0.5-31/70=35/70-31/70=4/70=2/35. dy=-√66/24+√66/35=√66(-1/24+1/35)=√66(-35+24)/(840)=√66(-11)/840=-11√66/840. So |O-E|^2 = (2/35)^2 + (11√66/840)^2 = 4/1225 + 121*66/705600 = 4/1225 + 7986/705600 = 4/1225 + 1331/117600? Because 7986/705600 simplify divide by 6: 1331/117600. Now, 4/1225 = 4*96/117600=384/117600. Sum= (384+1331)/117600=1715/117600=343/23520? 1715/5=343, 117600/5=23520, so 343/23520. And OB^2=7/480=7*49/480*49=343/23520. So they are equal. So O satisfies |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2. Therefore, our derived equation (1) should be satisfied by O. But we computed that Eq1' gives 0.11, not 0. So there is an error in the derivation of Eq1'.

Let's re-derive the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 from scratch with the numeric values to see what equation we get. We have B=(19/50, -√66/25). E=(31/70, -√66/35). Let O=(X,Y). Then:

|O-B|^2 = (X - 19/50)^2 + (Y + √66/25)^2.
|O-E|^2 = (X - 31/70)^2 + (Y + √66/35)^2.

Set equal:

(X - 19/50)^2 + (Y + √66/25)^2 = (X - 31/70)^2 + (Y + √66/35)^2.

Expand:

X^2 - (38/50)X + (19/50)^2 + Y^2 + (2√66/25)Y + (√66/25)^2 = X^2 - (62/70)X + (31/70)^2 + Y^2 + (2√66/35)Y + (√66/35)^2.

Cancel X^2, Y^2:

- (38/50)X + (19/50)^2 + (2√66/25)Y + (√66/25)^2 = - (62/70)X + (31/70)^2 + (2√66/35)Y + (√66/35)^2.

Bring terms:

- (38/50)X + (62/70)X + (2√66/25)Y - (2√66/35)Y + (19/50)^2 - (31/70)^2 + (√66/25)^2 - (√66/35)^2 = 0.

Compute X coefficient: -38/50 + 62/70 = -19/25 + 31/35 = common denominator 175: -19*7=-133, 31*5=155, sum 22/175. So X coefficient = 22/175.
Y coefficient: 2√66/25 - 2√66/35 = 2√66(1/25 - 1/35) = 2√66( (7-5)/175 ) = 2√66 * 2/175 = 4√66/175.
Constant: (19/50)^2 - (31/70)^2 = 361/2500 - 961/4900 = (361*49 - 961*25)/122500 = (17689 - 24025)/122500 = -6336/122500 = -6336/122500 = simplify divide by 4: -1584/30625.
(√66/25)^2 - (√66/35)^2 = 66/625 - 66/1225 = 66(1/625 - 1/1225) = 66( (1225-625)/(625*1225) ) = 66*600/765625 = 39600/765625 = simplify divide by 25: 1584/30625. So constant sum = -1584/30625 + 1584/30625 = 0.

Thus, the equation reduces to:

(22/175) X + (4√66/175) Y = 0 => Multiply by 175: 22 X + 4√66 Y = 0 => Divide by 2: 11 X + 2√66 Y = 0. So (EqA): 11 X + 2√66 Y = 0.

Now, from the numeric example, we have O=(1/2, -√66/24). Plug into EqA: 11*(1/2) + 2√66*(-√66/24) = 11/2 + 2*66*(-1/24) = 11/2 - 132/24 = 11/2 - 5.5 = 0. So O satisfies EqA. Good.

Now, our derived equation (1) from the general derivation was: t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0. For the numeric example, t_E v_x = (2/7)*(11/50)=22/350=11/175. So 11/175 * X. t_E v_y + y = (2/7)*(-6√66/25) + √66/25 = -12√66/175 + 7√66/175 = -5√66/175 = -√66/35. So the equation becomes (11/175) X + (-√66/35) Y = 0 => Multiply by 175: 11 X - 5√66 Y = 0? Because -√66/35 * 175 = -√66 * 5 = -5√66. So 11 X - 5√66 Y = 0. But the correct equation from the equality is 11 X + 2√66 Y = 0. So there is a sign discrepancy: In our derivation, we had (t_E v_y + y) O_y, but we computed t_E v_y + y = -√66/35, and O_y = -√66/24, so product = (-√66/35)*(-√66/24)=66/(840)=11/140? Actually, 66/840=11/140. Then (11/175)*X = (11/175)*(1/2)=11/350. Sum = 11/350+11/140=11/350+11/140=11/350+55/700? Let's compute: 11/350 = 11/350, 11/140=55/700=22/280? Not needed. So the sum is not zero; it's 11/350+11/140=11/350+55/700? Actually, 11/140 = 55/700, 11/350 = 22/700, sum=77/700=11/100=0.11. So the equation (11/175) X + (-√66/35) Y = 0 gives 11/175 * 1/2 + (-√66/35)*(-√66/24) = 11/350 + (66)/(840) = 11/350 + 11/140 = 11/350 + 55/700? Actually, 66/840 = 11/140, yes. So sum = 11/350 + 11/140 = 11/350 + 55/700? 11/140 = 55/700, 11/350 = 22/700, sum=77/700=11/100. Not zero. So the equation (t_E v_x) O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0 is not satisfied by O. Therefore, our derivation of that equation must be wrong. Let's re-derive the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 in general.

We have B = (x, -y). E = (x + t_E v_x, y + t_E v_y). The condition is:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - x - t_E v_x)^2 + (O_y - y - t_E v_y)^2.

Expand both sides:

Left: O_x^2 - 2x O_x + x^2 + O_y^2 + 2y O_y + y^2.
Right: O_x^2 - 2(x + t_E v_x) O_x + (x + t_E v_x)^2 + O_y^2 - 2(y + t_E v_y) O_y + (y + t_E v_y)^2.

Cancel O_x^2, O_y^2:

-2x O_x + x^2 + 2y O_y + y^2 = -2x O_x - 2 t_E v_x O_x + x^2 + 2x t_E v_x + t_E^2 v_x^2 -2y O_y - 2 t_E v_y O_y + y^2 + 2y t_E v_y + t_E^2 v_y^2.

Now, bring all terms to left:

-2x O_x + x^2 + 2y O_y + y^2 + 2x O_x + 2 t_E v_x O_x - x^2 - 2x t_E v_x - t_E^2 v_x^2 + 2y O_y + 2 t_E v_y O_y - y^2 - 2y t_E v_y - t_E^2 v_y^2 = 0.

Simplify: (-2x O_x + 2x O_x) = 0. (x^2 - x^2) = 0. (y^2 - y^2) = 0. So we get:

2 t_E v_x O_x + 2y O_y + 2 t_E v_y O_y - 2x t_E v_x - 2y t_E v_y - t_E^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = 0.

Thus:

2 t_E v_x O_x + 2 (t_E v_y + y) O_y - 2 t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - t_E^2 D = 0.

Divide by 2:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0.

So that is the correct equation. Now, we need to compute x v_x + y v_y. x v_x + y v_y = x(p-x) + y(q-y) = p x - x^2 + q y - y^2 = (p x + q y) - (x^2+y^2). We have p x + q y = -p r? Actually, we derived that from PA=PC: p x + q y = -p r. Let's check: p x + q y = p x + (-p(r+x)/y) y = p x - p(r+x) = p x - p r - p x = -p r. So that is correct. Then x v_x + y v_y = -p r - r^2 = -r(p+r). So t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = -t_E r(p+r). And (t_E^2/2) D = (t_E^2/2) D. But we also have t_E = 2r(p+r)/D, so t_E r(p+r) = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D, and (t_E^2/2) D = (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 * D = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So indeed, t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) D = -2r^2(p+r)^2/D + 2r^2(p+r)^2/D = 0. Thus, the equation reduces to:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0.   (1)

So this is correct. But for the numeric example, we computed t_E v_x = 11/175, t_E v_y + y = -√66/35, and O=(1/2, -√66/24). Plug into (1): (11/175)*(1/2) + (-√66/35)*(-√66/24) = 11/350 + 66/(840) = 11/350 + 11/140 = 11/350 + 55/700 = 22/700 + 55/700 = 77/700 = 11/100, not 0. So (1) is not satisfied. But we know that O satisfies |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2. Therefore, our equation (1) must be wrong because we made an algebraic mistake in the derivation of the simplified equation. Let's test the derivation of (1) using the numeric values directly: We have B=(19/50, -√66/25), E=(31/70, -√66/35). Let O=(X,Y). The equality condition gave 11X+2√66 Y=0. That is the correct equation. So we need to see if t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0 is equivalent to that. For the numeric values, t_E v_x = 11/175, t_E v_y + y = -√66/35. These are not the same unless Y=0. So the equation (1) is not equivalent to the correct condition. Therefore, our derivation that the constant term cancels must have an error: The constant term is -t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2)D. We claimed that t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2)D = 0. Now, t_E = 2/7 ≈0.2857. So t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = (2/7)*(-0.55)= -0.1571. And (t_E^2/2) D = ( (4/49)/2 ) * (77/20) = (2/49)*(77/20)= (2*77)/(49*20)= (2*11)/(7*20)= 22/140=0.1571. So sum = 0. So that part is correct. So the constant term is zero. Therefore, the equation should be t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y = 0. But this is not satisfied by O. -2x O_x + x^2 + 2y O_y + y^2 + 2x O_x + 2 t_E v_x O_x - x^2 - 2x t_E v_x - t_E^2 v_x^2 + 2y O_y + 2 t_E v_y O_y - y^2 - 2y t_E v_y - t_E^2 v_y^2 = 0.

Divide by 2:

O = (1/2, -√66/24). t_E v_x = (2/7)*(11/50)=22/350=11/175. So t_E v_x O_x = (11/175)*(1/2)=11/350.

Then -t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = 11/70.

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0, which gives 11/100 + 0 = 0? That would imply 11/100=0, which is false. So there is an inconsistency: The equality condition is supposed to hold for O, but when we plug O into the equation, we get 11/100=0. Therefore, the equation we derived from the equality must be wrong. Let's test the equality condition directly with O and see if it holds. We already did: |O-B|^2 = 7/480, |O-E|^2 = 7/480, so they are equal. So the equality condition is satisfied. Therefore, our derived equation must be equivalent to that condition, so it must hold for O. Since it doesn't, there must be a mistake in the derivation of the equation. Let's re-derive the equation from the equality with the numeric values step by step, but this time without simplifying the constant term. We'll compute the left and right sides of the equality for O and see what equation we get.

Set O = (X,Y). The equality is:

(X - 19/50)^2 + (Y + √66/25)^2 = (X - 31/70)^2 + (Y + √66/35)^2.

For X=1/2, Y=-√66/24, compute left side: (1/2 - 19/50)^2 + (-√66/24 + √66/25)^2. We already computed that this equals 7/480. Right side: (1/2 - 31/70)^2 + (-√66/24 + √66/35)^2. We computed that also equals 7/480. So they are equal. Therefore, the equation holds. Now, if we expand the equality, we should get an equation that is satisfied by O. Let's expand the equality and then substitute O to see if it yields 0=0. We already did the expansion and got (22/175)X + (4√66/175)Y = 0. That is the correct condition. So the equation is 11X+2√66 Y=0. Now, our derived equation from the general derivation was: t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = 0. Let's compute this for O using the exact values and see if it yields 0. We have t_E v_x O_x = (11/175)*(1/2)=11/350. (t_E v_y + y) O_y = (-√66/35)*(-√66/24)=66/840=11/140. Sum = 11/350+11/140 = 11/350+55/700 = 22/700+55/700=77/700=11/100. Then -t_E (x v_x + y v_y) - (t_E^2/2) D = - (2/7)*(-11/20) - (11/70) = (22/140) - (11/70) = (11/70) - (11/70)=0. So total = 11/100. So the equation gives 11/100=0, which is false. Therefore, the equation we derived is not equivalent to the equality condition. So there must be an error in the step where we derived that the equality condition simplifies to that equation. Let's re-derive the equation from the equality more carefully, using the symbols.

We have:

|O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2.

Write:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - x - t_E v_x)^2 + (O_y - y - t_E v_y)^2.

Expand the right side:

= (O_x - x - t_E v_x)^2 + (O_y - y - t_E v_y)^2.

Now, note that (O_x - x - t_E v_x) = (O_x - x) - t_E v_x. So square:

= (O_x - x)^2 - 2 (O_x - x) t_E v_x + t_E^2 v_x^2.

Similarly, (O_y - y - t_E v_y) = (O_y - y) - t_E v_y, square:

= (O_y - y)^2 - 2 (O_y - y) t_E v_y + t_E^2 v_y^2.

Thus, the right side becomes:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y - y)^2 - 2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2).

Now, the left side is:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2.

Set equal:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - x)^2 + (O_y - y)^2 - 2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 D.

Cancel (O_x - x)^2 from both sides:

(O_y + y)^2 = (O_y - y)^2 - 2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 D.

Now, expand (O_y + y)^2 = O_y^2 + 2y O_y + y^2. (O_y - y)^2 = O_y^2 - 2y O_y + y^2.

So:

O_y^2 + 2y O_y + y^2 = O_y^2 - 2y O_y + y^2 - 2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 D.

Cancel O_y^2 and y^2:

2y O_y = -2y O_y - 2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 D.

Bring terms:

2y O_y + 2y O_y = - 2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 D.

So:

4y O_y = - 2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 D.

Divide by 2:

2y O_y = - t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + (t_E^2/2) D.

Now, bring the term with O_y:

t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + 2y O_y = (t_E^2/2) D.

Expand:

t_E v_x O_x - t_E v_x x + t_E v_y O_y - t_E v_y y + 2y O_y = (t_E^2/2) D.

Thus:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = (t_E^2/2) D.

So the equation is:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = (t_E^2/2) D.

Now, this is different from our previous equation: we had (t_E v_y + y) O_y, but now it's (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y. Let's check the derivation. We had 4y O_y = -2 t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + t_E^2 D. Then divide by 2: 2y O_y = - t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + (t_E^2/2) D. Then bring the term: t_E [ (O_x - x) v_x + (O_y - y) v_y ] + 2y O_y = (t_E^2/2) D. Expand: t_E v_x O_x - t_E v_x x + t_E v_y O_y - t_E v_y y + 2y O_y = (t_E^2/2) D. So t_E v_x O_x + t_E v_y O_y + 2y O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = (t_E^2/2) D. So t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y - t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = (t_E^2/2) D. That is correct.

Thus, the correct equation from |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 is:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y = t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) D.   (1')

Now, we previously computed t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) D = 0? Let's check: t_E (x v_x + y v_y) = -t_E r(p+r) = -(2r(p+r)/D)*r(p+r) = -2r^2(p+r)^2/D. (t_E^2/2) D = (4r^2(p+r)^2/D^2)/2 * D = 2r^2(p+r)^2/D. So they cancel. So the right side is 0. Therefore, the equation simplifies to:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y = 0.   (1'')

Now, for the numeric example, t_E v_x = 11/175, t_E v_y + 2y = (-5√66/175) + 2*(√66/25) = -5√66/175 + 2√66/25 = -5√66/175 + 14√66/175 = 9√66/175. So the equation becomes (11/175)X + (9√66/175)Y = 0 => 11X + 9√66 Y = 0. For O=(1/2, -√66/24), compute 11*(1/2) + 9√66*(-√66/24) = 11/2 - 9*66/24 = 11/2 - 594/24 = 11/2 - 24.75 = 5.5 - 24.75 = -19.25, not 0. So still not satisfied. So there is an error: The constant term we computed as t_E (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_E^2/2) D = 0 is correct, but then the equation should be t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y = 0. However, for O, this sum is not zero. Therefore, the only conclusion is that the expansion of the equality is wrong, or we have made an algebraic mistake in the derivation of the equation. Let's recompute the expansion with the numeric values to see what equation we get directly from the equality condition.

We have O = (X,Y). The equality is:

(X - 19/50)^2 + (Y + √66/25)^2 = (X - 31/70)^2 + (Y + √66/35)^2.

We already did the expansion and got (22/175)X + (4√66/175)Y = 0. That is the correct condition. So we need to see if this can be expressed in terms of t_E, v_x, etc. Let's compute t_E v_x and t_E v_y + 2y for the numeric example.

t_E = 2/7. v_x = 11/50. So t_E v_x = (2/7)*(11/50)=22/350=11/175.
t_E v_y + 2y = (2/7)*(-6√66/25) + 2*(√66/25) = -12√66/175 + 2√66/25 = -12√66/175 + 14√66/175 = 2√66/175.
Thus, the equation t_E v_x X + (t_E v_y + 2y)Y = 0 becomes (11/175)X + (2√66/175)Y = 0 => 11X + 2√66 Y = 0. That is exactly the correct condition! Because we have 11X + 2√66 Y = 0. Thus, the correct general equation from |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 is:

t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y = 0.   (1)

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we will get:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + 2y) O_y = t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_F^2/2) D.

We need to derive that. Let's do it similarly.

We have B=(x, -y), F=(x + t_F v_x, y + t_F v_y). Then |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2 gives:

(O_x - x)^2 + (O_y + y)^2 = (O_x - x - t_F v_x)^2 + (O_y - y - t_F v_y)^2.

Following the same steps as above, we get:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + 2y) O_y - t_F (x v_x + y v_y) = (t_F^2/2) D.

So the equation is:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + 2y) O_y = t_F (x v_x + y v_y) + (t_F^2/2) D.   (2)

Now, we need to compute the right side. We have x v_x + y v_y = -r(p+r). So t_F (x v_x + y v_y) = -t_F r(p+r). And (t_F^2/2) D =? Since t_F = 2S/D, then (t_F^2/2) D = (4S^2/D^2)/2 * D = 2S^2/D. Also, t_F r(p+r) = (2S/D)*r(p+r) = 2S r(p+r)/D. So the right side becomes -2S r(p+r)/D + 2S^2/D = 2S( S - r(p+r) )/D. And we earlier computed that S - r(p+r) = -v_x. So the right side = -2S v_x / D = -t_F v_x. Therefore, equation (2) simplifies to:

t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + 2y) O_y = -t_F v_x.   (2')

Thus, we have the system:

(1) t_E v_x O_x + (t_E v_y + 2y) O_y = 0.
(2') t_F v_x O_x + (t_F v_y + 2y) O_y = -t_F v_x.

Now, this is a linear system in O_x, O_y. We can solve for O_x and O_y. Let's do it in general.

Let’s denote:

a = t_E v_x, b = t_E v_y + 2y.
c = t_F v_x, d = t_F v_y + 2y.
RHS = -t_F v_x = -c.

So the system is:

a O_x + b O_y = 0.
c O_x + d O_y = -c.

The determinant Δ = a d - b c = t_E v_x (t_F v_y + 2y) - (t_E v_y + 2y) t_F v_x = t_E v_x t_F v_y + 2 t_E v_x y - t_E v_y t_F v_x - 2y t_F v_x = 2 t_E v_x y - 2y t_F v_x = 2y v_x (t_E - t_F).

Thus, Δ = 2y v_x (t_E - t_F).

Now, solve for O_x:

And O_y = (a * (-c) - 0 * c) / Δ = -a c / Δ = - t_E v_x * t_F v_x / (2y v_x (t_E - t_F)) = - t_E t_F v_x / (2y (t_E - t_F)).

Thus, O_x = t_F (t_E v_y + 2y) / (2y (t_E - t_F)). O_y = - t_E t_F v_x / (2y (t_E - t_F)).

Better approach: Since C and D are on x-axis, the circumcenter P has coordinates ( (d+R-r)/2, y_P ) and lies on perpendicular bisector of AC. Let's do that:

Let x_P = (d+R-r)/2.

Compute:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand:

(x_P^2 -2x_P x_A + x_A^2) + (y_P^2 -2y_P y_A + y_A^2) = (x_P^2 +2r x_P + r^2) + y_P^2.

Cancel x_P^2 and y_P^2:

-2x_P x_A + x_A^2 -2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

So bring terms:

-2x_P x_A -2y_P y_A + (x_A^2+y_A^2) = 2r x_P + r^2.

But x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2 (since A on Ω). So:

-2x_P x_A -2y_P y_A + r^2 = 2r x_P + r^2 => cancel r^2:

-2x_P x_A -2y_P y_A = 2r x_P => divide 2: -x_P x_A - y_P y_A = r x_P => so

y_P y_A = -x_P x_A - r x_P = -x_P (x_A + r).

Thus y_P = -x_P (x_A + r)/y_A.

Alternatively, we can use complex numbers with the line MN as real axis. Let M=0, N=1 (after scaling). Then circles: |z| = r, |z-1| = R. Intersection points A and B: A = a, B = conjugate? Now P is circumcenter of triangle A, C, D. C = -r (real), D = 1+R (real). So we have three points: A (complex), C (real), D (real). The circumcenter is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. In complex numbers, the circumcenter of points z1, z2, z3 is given by formula: (|z1|^2 (z2 - z3) + |z2|^2 (z3 - z1) + |z3|^2 (z1 - z2)) / (z1 (z2 - z3) + z2 (z3 - z1) + z3 (z1 - z2))? Actually, there is formula using determinants. But maybe easier: Since C and D are real, the perpendicular bisector of CD is the vertical line at x = (C+D)/2 = (1+R - r)/2. So the circumcenter P has real part = (1+R - r)/2, and its imaginary part is something. Let’s denote P = p + i q, where p = (1+R - r)/2. And q = Im(P). We can find q by using that |P - A| = |P - C|. So |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That yields:

(P - A)(\overline{P} - \overline{A}) = (P - C)(\overline{P} - C). Since C real, \overline{C}=C. So:

|P|^2 - P \overline{A} - \overline{P} A + |A|^2 = |P|^2 - P C - \overline{P} C + C^2 => cancel |P|^2:

- P \overline{A} - \overline{P} A + |A|^2 = - P C - \overline{P} C + C^2 => bring terms:

- P \overline{A} - \overline{P} A + P C + \overline{P} C = C^2 - |A|^2 => P (C - \overline{A}) + \overline{P} (C - A) = C^2 - r^2 (since |A|^2=r^2). This is a linear equation in P and \overline{P}. Since P is complex, we can separate real and imaginary parts. But note that A is not necessarily real; we have A = x+iy. Then \overline{A}=x-iy. So C - \overline{A} = C - x + iy, and C - A = C - x - iy. So P (C - x + iy) + \overline{P} (C - x - iy) = C^2 - r^2. This is equivalent to: 2 Re[ P (C - x + iy) ]? Actually, P (C - x + iy) + \overline{P} (C - x - iy) = 2 Re[ P (C - x + iy) ]. So we have:

2 Re[ P (C - x + iy) ] = C^2 - r^2.

Now P = p + iq. So P (C - x + iy) = (p+iq)(C-x+iy) = p(C-x) + i p y + i q (C-x) - q y = [p(C-x)-q y] + i[ p y + q(C-x) ]. Its real part = p(C-x) - q y. So condition: 2(p(C-x)-q y) = C^2 - r^2.

Thus p(C-x) - q y = (C^2 - r^2)/2. So q = (p(C-x) - (C^2 - r^2)/2)/y.

Set d=1. Use coordinates:

Now line AP: direction v = (p-x, q-y). We'll compute t_E and t_F.

First, compute v·A = (p-x)x + (q-y)y = p x - x^2 + q y - y^2 = p x + q y - (x^2+y^2) = p x + q y - r^2.

Now we need p x + q y. We have q = -p(r+x)/y. So q y = -p(r+x). So p x + q y = p x - p(r+x) = p x - p r - p x = -p r. So p x + q y = -p r. Therefore, v·A = -p r - r^2 = -r(p + r). So v·A = -r(p+r). Good!

Thus t_E = -2 (v·A)/|v|^2 = -2 * (-r(p+r))/|v|^2 = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2.

So E = A + t_E v = A + (2r(p+r)/|v|^2) v.

Now compute v·(A-N). Since N=(1,0), A-N = (x-1, y). Then v·(A-N) = (p-x)(x-1) + (q-y)y = (p-x)(x-1) + q y - y^2 = (p-x)(x-1) + q y - y^2.

We can compute q y = -p(r+x). And y^2 = r^2 - x^2. So:

v·(A-N) = (p-x)(x-1) - p(r+x) - (r^2 - x^2) = (p-x)(x-1) - p r - p x - r^2 + x^2.

Now expand (p-x)(x-1) = p(x-1) - x(x-1) = p x - p - x^2 + x. So:

Better: Let L: H + s v. The foot of perpendicular from O to L is some point T; the condition is |O - T| = radius. But we can also compute the discriminant of the quadratic in s for the intersection: (H + s v - O)^2 = R^2 => |v|^2 s^2 + 2 v·(H-O) s + |H-O|^2 - R^2 = 0. This quadratic has discriminant Δ = 4[ v·(H-O) ]^2 - 4|v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2). For tangency, Δ=0, i.e., [v·(H-O)]^2 = |v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2). So we need to show that this holds for O, the circumcenter of BEF. But we don't know O explicitly. However, maybe we can compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and also the dot product v·(H-O) in terms of known quantities. Alternatively, we can use the fact that the circle (BEF) is determined by points B, E, F; we might find that H is the orthocenter of triangle something, and that the line through H parallel to AP is the tangent to (BEF) at some point that is the reflection of something.

Given the time, we can try to compute O explicitly. Let’s set up coordinates in the plane with M=(0,0), N=(1,0). We have:

A = (x, y), B = (x, -y).
P = (p, q), with p = (1+R-r)/2, q = -p(r+x)/y.
H = (p, y_H) with y_H = - (1-p) y/(r+x).

v = (p-x, q-y). We have p-x = (R-r)(R+r+1)/2, positive.
And q-y = -p(r+x)/y - y = -(p(r+x)+y^2)/y.

Now, we also have t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2, t_F = 2(p(1+r)+r^2 - x)/|v|^2. Note that |v|^2 = (p+r)^2+q^2.

We can compute E = (x,y) + t_E (p-x, q-y), F = (x,y) + t_F (p-x, q-y).

Now, we need circle (BEF). Let's find the circumcenter O. Since E and F lie on line AP, the perpendicular bisector of EF is a line perpendicular to v through the midpoint M_EF = (x,y) + ((t_E+t_F)/2) v. That is, point O lies on line: O = M_EF + λ n, where n is a unit vector perpendicular to v (say n = (q-y, -(p-x)) normalized). Also, O lies on perpendicular bisector of BE. So we can compute O as intersection of these two lines. But maybe we can find O directly by using the fact that the circle passes through B and the two points on line L. There is a known formula: For a circle through B and two points on a line L (through A), the center is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF and the perpendicular bisector of BE. We can compute O using coordinates. This might be messy but we can try to simplify by using algebraic identities.

For B: x^2+y^2 + Dx + E(-y) + F = 0 => x^2+y^2 + Dx - Ey + F = 0. (1)
For E: x_E^2+y_E^2 + Dx_E + Ey_E + F = 0. (2)
For F: x_F^2+y_F^2 + Dx_F + Ey_F + F = 0. (3)

We can solve for D, E, F. Since E and F are on line AP, we can express them in terms of A and v.
